Inside the safe were various items, including a large glass bottle containing a whitish slurry which was subsequently identified as the oldest sample of weapons-grade plutonium known to exist. Isotope analysis by <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest_National_Laboratory" title="Pacific Northwest National Laboratory">Pacific Northwest National Laboratory</a> indicated that the plutonium in the bottle was manufactured in the X-10 Graphite Reactor at Oak Ridge during 1944.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trinity_and_Fat_Man_atomic_bombs">Trinity and Fat Man atomic bombs</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fission_bomb_assembly_methods.svg"><img alt='Two diagrams of weapon assembly. Top: "gun-type assembly method" — an elliptical shell encloses conventional chemical explosives on the left, whose detonation pushes sub-critical pieces of uranium-235 together on the right. Bottom: "implosion assembly method" — a spherical shell encloses eight high-explosive charges which upon detonation compress a plutonium charge in the core.' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="498" data-file-width="428" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Fission_bomb_assembly_methods.svg/220px-Fission_bomb_assembly_methods.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Fission_bomb_assembly_methods.svg/330px-Fission_bomb_assembly_methods.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Fission_bomb_assembly_methods.svg/440px-Fission_bomb_assembly_methods.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fission_bomb_assembly_methods.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Because of the presence of plutonium-240 in reactor-bred plutonium, the implosion design was developed for the "<a href="/wiki/Fat_Man" title="Fat Man">Fat Man</a>" and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinity_test" title="Trinity test">Trinity</a>" weapons</div></div></div>
<p>The first atomic bomb test, codenamed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinity_test" title="Trinity test">"Trinity"</a> and detonated on July 16, 1945, near <a href="/wiki/Alamogordo,_New_Mexico" title="Alamogordo, New Mexico">Alamogordo, New Mexico</a>, used plutonium as its fissile material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miner1968p541_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-Miner1968p541-43">[42]</a></sup> The implosion design of "<a href="/wiki/Trinity_(nuclear_test)#The_gadget" title="Trinity (nuclear test)">the gadget</a>", as the Trinity device was code-named, used conventional explosive lenses to compress a sphere of plutonium into a supercritical mass, which was simultaneously showered with neutrons from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urchin_(detonator)" title="Urchin (detonator)">"Urchin"</a>, an initiator made of <a href="/wiki/Polonium" title="Polonium">polonium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a> (<a href="/wiki/Neutron_source" title="Neutron source">neutron source</a>: <a href="/wiki/Neutron_source#Small_sized_devices" title="Neutron source">(α, n) reaction</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001_32-8"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001-32">[31]</a></sup> Together, these ensured a runaway chain reaction and explosion. The overall weapon weighed over 4 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>, although it used just 6.2 kg of plutonium in its core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[81]</a></sup> About 20% of the plutonium used in the Trinity weapon underwent fission, resulting in an explosion with an energy equivalent to approximately 20,000 tons of TNT.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yield_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-yield-88">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[note 7]</a></sup>
</p><p>An identical design was used in the "Fat Man" atomic bomb dropped on <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki" title="Nagasaki">Nagasaki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, on August 9, 1945, killing 35,000–40,000 people and destroying 68%–80% of war production at Nagasaki.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESklar198422–29_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESklar198422–29-91">[84]</a></sup> Only after the announcement of the first atomic bombs was the existence and name of plutonium made known to the public by the Manhattan Project's <a href="/wiki/Smyth_Report" title="Smyth Report">Smyth Report</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBernstein200770_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBernstein200770-92">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cold_War_use_and_waste">Cold War use and waste</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Reactor-grade_plutonium" title="Reactor-grade plutonium">Reactor-grade plutonium</a></div>
<p>Large stockpiles of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weapons-grade_plutonium" title="Weapons-grade plutonium">weapons-grade plutonium</a> were built up by both the Soviet Union and the United States during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>. The U.S. reactors at Hanford and the <a href="/wiki/Savannah_River_Site" title="Savannah River Site">Savannah River Site</a> in South Carolina produced 103 tonnes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[86]</a></sup> and an estimated 170 tonnes of military-grade plutonium was produced in the USSR.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[note 8]</a></sup> Each year about 20 tonnes of the element is still produced as a by-product of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC2006p4-27_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2006p4-27-10">[10]</a></sup> As much as 1000 tonnes of plutonium may be in storage with more than 200 tonnes of that either inside or extracted from nuclear weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001_32-9"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001-32">[31]</a></sup>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SIPRI" title="SIPRI">SIPRI</a> estimated the world plutonium <a href="/wiki/Stockpile" title="Stockpile">stockpile</a> in 2007 as about 500 tonnes, divided equally between weapon and civilian stocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStockholm_International_Peace_Research_Institute2007567_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStockholm_International_Peace_Research_Institute2007567-97">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>Radioactive contamination at the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Flats_Plant" title="Rocky Flats Plant">Rocky Flats Plant</a> primarily resulted from two major plutonium fires in 1957 and 1969. Much lower concentrations of radioactive isotopes were released throughout the operational life of the plant from 1952 to 1992. Prevailing winds from the plant carried airborne contamination south and east, into populated areas northwest of Denver. The contamination of the Denver area by plutonium from the fires and other sources was not publicly reported until the 1970s. According to a 1972 study coauthored by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Martell" title="Edward Martell">Edward Martell</a>, "In the more densely populated areas of Denver, the Pu contamination level in surface soils is several times fallout", and the plutonium contamination "just east of the Rocky Flats plant ranges up to hundreds of times that from nuclear tests".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poet1972_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poet1972-98">[90]</a></sup> As noted by <a href="/wiki/Carl_J._Johnson" title="Carl J. Johnson">Carl Johnson</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambio" title="Ambio">Ambio</a>, "Exposures of a large population in the Denver area to plutonium and other radionuclides in the exhaust plumes from the plant date back to 1953."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson1981_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson1981-99">[91]</a></sup> Weapons production at the Rocky Flats plant was halted after a combined <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">FBI</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">EPA</a> raid in 1989 and years of protests. The plant has since been shut down, with its buildings demolished and completely removed from the site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FWSmainpage_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FWSmainpage-100">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the U.S., some plutonium extracted from dismantled nuclear weapons is melted to form glass logs of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plutonium_oxide" title="Plutonium oxide">plutonium oxide</a> that weigh two tonnes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001_32-10"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001-32">[31]</a></sup> The glass is made of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Borosilicate" title="Borosilicate">borosilicates</a> mixed with <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gadolinium" title="Gadolinium">gadolinium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[note 9]</a></sup> These logs are planned to be encased in <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">stainless steel</a> and stored as much as 4 km (2 mi) underground in bore holes that will be back-filled with <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001_32-11"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001-32">[31]</a></sup> The U.S. planned to store plutonium in this way at the <a href="/wiki/Yucca_Mountain_nuclear_waste_repository" title="Yucca Mountain nuclear waste repository">Yucca Mountain nuclear waste repository</a>, which is about 100 miles (160 km) north-east of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Las_Vegas,_Nevada" title="Las Vegas, Nevada">Las Vegas, Nevada</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>On March 5, 2009, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Energy" title="United States Secretary of Energy">Energy Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Steven_Chu" title="Steven Chu">Steven Chu</a> told a Senate hearing "the Yucca Mountain site no longer was viewed as an option for storing reactor waste".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[94]</a></sup> Starting in 1999, military-generated nuclear waste is being entombed at the <a href="/wiki/Waste_Isolation_Pilot_Plant" title="Waste Isolation Pilot Plant">Waste Isolation Pilot Plant</a> in New Mexico.
</p><p>In a Presidential Memorandum dated January 29, 2010, President Obama established the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Ribbon_Commission_on_America%27s_Nuclear_Future" title="Blue Ribbon Commission on America's Nuclear Future">Blue Ribbon Commission on America's Nuclear Future</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-About_the_Commission_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-About_the_Commission-104">[95]</a></sup> In their final report the Commission put forth recommendations for developing a comprehensive strategy to pursue, including:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blue_Ribbon_Commission_on_America's_Nuclear_Future_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blue_Ribbon_Commission_on_America's_Nuclear_Future-105">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>"Recommendation #1: The United States should undertake an integrated nuclear waste management program that leads to the timely development of one or more permanent deep geological facilities for the safe disposal of spent fuel and high-level nuclear waste".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blue_Ribbon_Commission_on_America's_Nuclear_Future_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blue_Ribbon_Commission_on_America's_Nuclear_Future-105">[96]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_experimentation">Medical experimentation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Human_radiation_experiments" title="Human radiation experiments">Human radiation experiments</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Stevens" title="Albert Stevens">Albert Stevens</a></div>
<p>During and after the end of World War II, scientists working on the Manhattan Project and other nuclear weapons research projects conducted studies of the effects of plutonium on laboratory animals and human subjects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Injection_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Injection-106">[97]</a></sup> Animal studies found that a few milligrams of plutonium per kilogram of tissue is a lethal dose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PuHealth_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-PuHealth-107">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the case of human subjects, this involved injecting solutions containing (typically) five micrograms of plutonium into hospital patients thought to be either terminally ill, or to have a life expectancy of less than ten years either due to age or chronic disease condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Injection_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Injection-106">[97]</a></sup> This was reduced to one microgram in July 1945 after animal studies found that the way plutonium distributed itself in bones was more dangerous than <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PuHealth_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-PuHealth-107">[98]</a></sup> Most of the subjects, <a href="/wiki/Eileen_Welsome" title="Eileen Welsome">Eileen Welsome</a> says, were poor, powerless, and sick.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rc_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-rc-108">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1945 to 1947, eighteen human test subjects were injected with plutonium without <a href="/wiki/Informed_consent" title="Informed consent">informed consent</a>. The tests were used to create diagnostic tools to determine the uptake of plutonium in the body in order to develop safety standards for working with plutonium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Injection_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-Injection-106">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ebb_Cade" title="Ebb Cade">Ebb Cade</a> was an unwilling participant in medical experiments that involved injection of 4.7 micrograms of Plutonium on 10 April 1945 at <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge,_Tennessee" title="Oak Ridge, Tennessee">Oak Ridge, Tennessee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[101]</a></sup> This experiment was under the supervision of <a href="/wiki/Harold_Hodge" title="Harold Hodge">Harold Hodge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[102]</a></sup> Other experiments directed by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Atomic_Energy_Commission" title="United States Atomic Energy Commission">United States Atomic Energy Commission</a> and the Manhattan Project continued into the 1970s. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Plutonium_Files" title="The Plutonium Files">The Plutonium Files</a></i> chronicles the lives of the subjects of the secret program by naming each person involved and discussing the ethical and medical research conducted in secret by the scientists and doctors. The episode is now considered to be a serious breach of <a href="/wiki/Medical_ethics" title="Medical ethics">medical ethics</a> and of the <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Oath" title="Hippocratic Oath">Hippocratic Oath</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>The government covered up most of these radiation mishaps until 1993, when President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> ordered a change of policy and federal agencies then made available relevant records. The resulting investigation was undertaken by the president’s <a href="/wiki/Advisory_Committee_on_Human_Radiation_Experiments" title="Advisory Committee on Human Radiation Experiments">Advisory Committee on Human Radiation Experiments</a>, and it uncovered much of the material about plutonium research on humans. The committee issued a controversial 1995 report which said that "wrongs were committed" but it did not condemn those who perpetrated them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rc_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-rc-108">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Explosives">Explosives</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nagasakibomb.jpg"><img alt="Photo of an atomic explosion mushroom cloud with a gray stem and white cap" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3877" data-file-width="3245" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Nagasakibomb.jpg/170px-Nagasakibomb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Nagasakibomb.jpg/255px-Nagasakibomb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Nagasakibomb.jpg/340px-Nagasakibomb.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nagasakibomb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">atomic bomb dropped on Nagasaki, Japan</a> in 1945 had a plutonium core.</div></div></div>
<p>The isotope plutonium-239 is a key fissile component in nuclear weapons, due to its ease of fission and availability. Encasing the bomb's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plutonium_pit" title="Plutonium pit">plutonium pit</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon_design" title="Nuclear weapon design">tamper</a> (an optional layer of dense material) decreases the amount of plutonium needed to reach <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_mass_(nuclear)" title="Critical mass (nuclear)">critical mass</a> by <a href="/wiki/Neutron_reflector" title="Neutron reflector">reflecting escaping neutrons</a> back into the plutonium core. This reduces the amount of plutonium needed to reach criticality from 16 kg to 10 kg, which is a sphere with a diameter of about 10 centimeters (4 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartin2000532_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartin2000532-113">[104]</a></sup> This critical mass is about a third of that for uranium-235.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heiserman1992_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-Heiserman1992-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Fat Man plutonium bombs used explosive compression of plutonium to obtain significantly higher densities than normal, combined with a central neutron source to begin the reaction and increase efficiency. Thus only 6.2 kg of plutonium was needed for an <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon_yield" title="Nuclear weapon yield">explosive yield</a> equivalent to 20 kilotons of TNT.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yield_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-yield-88">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FASdesign_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FASdesign-114">[105]</a></sup> Hypothetically, as little as 4 kg of plutonium—and maybe even less—could be used to make a single atomic bomb using very sophisticated assembly designs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FASdesign_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-FASdesign-114">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mixed_oxide_fuel">Mixed oxide fuel</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">Nuclear reprocessing</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">Spent nuclear fuel</a> from normal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_water_reactor" title="Light water reactor">light water reactors</a> contains plutonium, but it is a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-242" title="Plutonium-242">plutonium-242</a>, 240, 239 and 238. The mixture is not sufficiently enriched for efficient nuclear weapons, but can be used once as <a href="/wiki/MOX_fuel" title="MOX fuel">MOX fuel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MOX_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-MOX-115">[106]</a></sup> Accidental neutron capture causes the amount of plutonium-242 and 240 to grow each time the plutonium is irradiated in a reactor with low-speed "thermal" neutrons, so that after the second cycle, the plutonium can only be consumed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fast_neutron_reactor" title="Fast neutron reactor">fast neutron reactors</a>. If fast neutron reactors are not available (the normal case), excess plutonium is usually discarded, and forms the longest-lived component of nuclear waste. The desire to consume this plutonium and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transuranic" title="Transuranic">transuranic</a> fuels and reduce the radiotoxicity of the waste is the usual reason nuclear engineers give to make fast neutron reactors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETillChang2011254–256_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETillChang2011254–256-116">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most common chemical process, <a href="/wiki/PUREX" title="PUREX">PUREX</a> (<i>P</i>lutonium–<i>UR</i>anium <i>EX</i>traction) <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">reprocesses</a> spent nuclear fuel to extract plutonium and uranium which can be used to form a mixed oxide (MOX) fuel for reuse in nuclear reactors. Weapons-grade plutonium can be added to the fuel mix. MOX fuel is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_water_reactor" title="Light water reactor">light water reactors</a> and consists of 60 kg of plutonium per tonne of fuel; after four years, three-quarters of the plutonium is burned (turned into other elements).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001_32-12"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001-32">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Breeder_reactor" title="Breeder reactor">Breeder reactors</a> are specifically designed to create more fissionable material than they consume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETillChang201115_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETillChang201115-117">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>MOX fuel has been in use since the 1980s, and is widely used in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MOX_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-MOX-115">[106]</a></sup> In September 2000, the United States and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Federation" title="Russian Federation">Russian Federation</a> signed a <a href="/wiki/Plutonium_Management_and_Disposition_Agreement" title="Plutonium Management and Disposition Agreement">Plutonium Management and Disposition Agreement</a> by which each agreed to dispose of 34 tonnes of weapons-grade plutonium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USMOX_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-USMOX-118">[109]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Energy" title="U.S. Department of Energy">U.S. Department of Energy</a> plans to dispose of 34 tonnes of weapons-grade plutonium in the United States before the end of 2019 by converting the plutonium to a MOX fuel to be used in commercial nuclear power reactors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USMOX_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-USMOX-118">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>MOX fuel improves total burnup. A fuel rod is reprocessed after three years of use to remove waste products, which by then account for 3% of the total weight of the rods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001_32-13"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001-32">[31]</a></sup> Any uranium or plutonium isotopes produced during those three years are left and the rod goes back into production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[note 10]</a></sup> The presence of up to 1% gallium per mass in weapons-grade <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plutonium-gallium_alloy" title="Plutonium-gallium alloy">plutonium alloy</a> has the potential to interfere with long-term operation of a light water reactor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plutonium recovered from spent reactor fuel poses little proliferation hazard, because of excessive contamination with non-fissile plutonium-240 and plutonium-242. Separation of the isotopes is not feasible. A dedicated reactor operating on very low <a href="/wiki/Burnup" title="Burnup">burnup</a> (hence minimal exposure of newly formed plutonium-239 to additional neutrons which causes it to be transformed to heavier isotopes of plutonium) is generally required to produce material suitable for use in efficient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons" title="Nuclear weapons">nuclear weapons</a>. While "weapons-grade" plutonium is defined to contain at least 92% plutonium-239 (of the total plutonium), the United States have managed to detonate an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reactor-grade_plutonium_nuclear_test" title="Reactor-grade plutonium nuclear test">under-20Kt device</a> using plutonium believed to contain only about 85% plutonium-239, so called '"fuel-grade" plutonium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inf15_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-inf15-121">[111]</a></sup> The "reactor-grade" plutonium produced by a regular LWR burnup cycle typically contains less than 60% Pu-239, with up to 30% parasitic Pu-240/Pu-242, and 10–15% fissile Pu-241.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inf15_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-inf15-121">[111]</a></sup> It is unknown if a device using plutonium obtained from reprocessed civil nuclear waste can be detonated, however such a device could hypothetically fizzle and spread radioactive materials over a large urban area. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAEA" title="IAEA">IAEA</a> conservatively classifies plutonium of all isotopic vectors as "direct-use" material, that is, "nuclear material that can be used for the manufacture of nuclear explosives components without transmutation or further enrichment".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inf15_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-inf15-121">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_and_heat_source">Power and heat source</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plutonium_pellet.jpg"><img alt="Glowing cylinder of plutonium oxide standing in a circular pit" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="3200" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Plutonium_pellet.jpg/220px-Plutonium_pellet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Plutonium_pellet.jpg/330px-Plutonium_pellet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Plutonium_pellet.jpg/440px-Plutonium_pellet.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plutonium_pellet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A glowing cylinder of <sup>238</sup>PuO<sub>2</sub></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fueling_of_the_MSL_MMRTG_001.jpg"><img alt="Glowing graphite cube containing plutonium-238 oxide" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Fueling_of_the_MSL_MMRTG_001.jpg/220px-Fueling_of_the_MSL_MMRTG_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Fueling_of_the_MSL_MMRTG_001.jpg/330px-Fueling_of_the_MSL_MMRTG_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Fueling_of_the_MSL_MMRTG_001.jpg/440px-Fueling_of_the_MSL_MMRTG_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fueling_of_the_MSL_MMRTG_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <sup>238</sup>PuO<sub>2</sub> radioisotope thermoelectric generator of the <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i> rover</a></div></div></div>
<p>The isotope plutonium-238 has a half-life of 87.74 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[112]</a></sup> It emits a large amount of <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> with low levels of both <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a>/<a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> and spontaneous neutron rays/particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pumper_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-pumper-123">[113]</a></sup> Being an alpha emitter, it combines high energy radiation with low penetration and thereby requires minimal shielding. A sheet of paper can be used to shield against the alpha particles emitted by plutonium-238. One <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kilogram</a> of the isotope can generate about 570 watts of heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heiserman1992_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-Heiserman1992-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pumper_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-pumper-123">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>These characteristics make it well-suited for electrical power generation for devices that must function without direct maintenance for timescales approximating a human lifetime. It is therefore used in <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_heater_unit" title="Radioisotope heater unit">radioisotope heater units</a> such as those in the <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">Cassini</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solar_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Solar-124">[114]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">Galileo</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[115]</a></sup> space probes, and the <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)">Curiosity</a> <a href="/wiki/Mars_rover" title="Mars rover">Mars rover</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>The twin Voyager spacecraft were launched in 1977, each containing a 500 watt plutonium power source. Over 30 years later, each source is still producing about 300 watts which allows limited operation of each spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[117]</a></sup> An earlier version of the same technology powered five <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ALSEP" title="ALSEP">Apollo Lunar Surface Experiment Packages</a>, starting with <a href="/wiki/Apollo_12" title="Apollo 12">Apollo 12</a> in 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001_32-14"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plutonium-238 has also been used successfully to power artificial heart <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_pacemaker" title="Artificial pacemaker">pacemakers</a>, to reduce the risk of repeated surgery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[119]</a></sup> It has been largely replaced by lithium-based <a href="/wiki/Primary_cell" title="Primary cell">primary cells</a>, but as of  2003<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Plutonium&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> there were somewhere between 50 and 100 plutonium-powered pacemakers still implanted and functioning in living patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[120]</a></sup> Plutonium-238 was studied as a way to provide supplemental heat to <a href="/wiki/Scuba_diving" title="Scuba diving">scuba diving</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[121]</a></sup> Plutonium-238 mixed with beryllium is used to generate neutrons for research purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001_32-15"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Plutonium_in_the_environment" title="Plutonium in the environment">Plutonium in the environment</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">Toxicity</span></h3>
<p>There are two aspects to the harmful effects of plutonium: the radioactivity and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_(chemistry)#Detrimental_effects" title="Heavy metal (chemistry)">heavy metal poison</a> effects. Isotopes and compounds of plutonium are radioactive and accumulate in <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a>. Contamination by plutonium oxide has resulted from <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_nuclear_disasters_and_radioactive_incidents" title="Lists of nuclear disasters and radioactive incidents">nuclear disasters and radioactive incidents</a>, including military nuclear accidents where nuclear weapons have burned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[122]</a></sup> Studies of the effects of these smaller releases, as well as of the widespread radiation poisoning sickness and death following the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki</a>, have provided considerable information regarding the dangers, symptoms and prognosis of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiation_poisoning" title="Radiation poisoning">radiation poisoning</a>, which in the case of the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Hibakusha" title="Hibakusha">Hibakusha</a>/survivors was largely unrelated to direct plutonium exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the decay of plutonium, three types of radiation are released—alpha, beta, and gamma. Alpha, beta, and gamma radiation are all forms of <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a>. Either acute or longer-term exposure carries a danger of <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation#Biological_effects" title="Ionizing radiation">serious health outcomes</a> including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiation_sickness" title="Radiation sickness">radiation sickness</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_damage" title="Genetic damage">genetic damage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>, and death. The danger increases with the amount of exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001_32-16"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001-32">[31]</a></sup> Alpha radiation can travel only a short distance and cannot travel through the outer, dead layer of human skin. Beta radiation can penetrate human skin, but cannot go all the way through the body. Gamma radiation can go all the way through the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[124]</a></sup>
Even though alpha radiation cannot penetrate the skin, ingested or inhaled plutonium does irradiate internal organs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001_32-17"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001-32">[31]</a></sup> Alpha particles generated by inhaled plutonium have been found to cause lung cancer in a cohort of European nuclear workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[125]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeleton</a>, where plutonium accumulates, and the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>, where it collects and becomes concentrated, are at risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miner1968p545_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-Miner1968p545-31">[30]</a></sup> Plutonium is not absorbed into the body efficiently when ingested; only 0.04% of plutonium oxide is absorbed after ingestion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001_32-18"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001-32">[31]</a></sup> Plutonium absorbed by the body is excreted very slowly, with a <a href="/wiki/Biological_half-life" title="Biological half-life">biological half-life</a> of 200 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[126]</a></sup> Plutonium passes only slowly through cell membranes and intestinal boundaries, so absorption by ingestion and incorporation into bone structure proceeds very slowly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CohenMyth_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-CohenMyth-137">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plutonium is more dangerous when inhaled than when ingested. The risk of <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a> increases once the total radiation <a href="/wiki/Equivalent_dose" title="Equivalent dose">dose equivalent</a> of inhaled plutonium exceeds 400 <a href="/wiki/Sievert" title="Sievert">mSv</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-139">[129]</a></sup> The U.S. Department of Energy estimates that the lifetime cancer risk from inhaling 5,000 plutonium particles, each about 3 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%9Cm" title="Μm">µm</a> wide, to be 1% over the background U.S. average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world-nuclear_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-world-nuclear-140">[130]</a></sup> Ingestion or inhalation of large amounts may cause acute radiation poisoning and possibly death. However no human being is known to have died because of inhaling or ingesting plutonium, and many people have measurable amounts of plutonium in their bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inf15_121-3"><a href="#cite_note-inf15-121">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>The "<a href="/wiki/Hot_particle" title="Hot particle">hot particle</a>" theory in which a particle of plutonium dust irradiates a localized spot of lung tissue is not supported by mainstream research—such particles are more mobile than originally thought and toxicity is not measurably increased due to particulate form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CohenMyth_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-CohenMyth-137">[127]</a></sup> When inhaled, plutonium can pass into the bloodstream. Once in the bloodstream, plutonium moves throughout the body and into the bones, liver, or other body organs. Plutonium that reaches body organs generally stays in the body for decades and continues to expose the surrounding tissue to radiation and thus may cause cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA-141">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>A commonly cited quote by <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Nader" title="Ralph Nader">Ralph Nader</a> states that a pound of plutonium dust spread into the atmosphere would be enough to kill 8 billion people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[132]</a></sup> However, calculations show that one pound of plutonium could kill no more than 2 million people by inhalation. This makes the toxicity of plutonium roughly equivalent with that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nerve_gas" title="Nerve gas">nerve gas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen-13_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen-13-143">[133]</a></sup> Nader's views were challenged in 1976 by <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Cohen_(physicist)" title="Bernard Cohen (physicist)">Bernard Cohen</a>, as described in the book <i>Nuclear Power, Both Sides: The Best Arguments for and Against the Most Controversial Technology</i>. Cohen's own estimate is that a dose of 200 micrograms would likely be necessary to cause cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKakuTrainer198377_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKakuTrainer198377-144">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several populations of people who have been exposed to plutonium dust (e.g. people living down-wind of Nevada test sites, Nagasaki survivors, nuclear facility workers, and "terminally ill" patients injected with Pu in 1945–46 to study Pu metabolism) have been carefully followed and analyzed. These studies generally do not show especially high plutonium toxicity or plutonium-induced cancer results, such as <a href="/wiki/Albert_Stevens" title="Albert Stevens">Albert Stevens</a> who survived into old age after being injected with plutonium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CohenMyth_137-2"><a href="#cite_note-CohenMyth-137">[127]</a></sup> "There were about 25 workers from Los Alamos National Laboratory who inhaled a considerable amount of plutonium dust during 1940s; according to the hot-particle theory, each of them has a 99.5% chance of being dead from lung cancer by now, but there has not been a single lung cancer among them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen-13_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen-13-143">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plutonium has a metallic taste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWelsome200017_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWelsome200017-146">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticality_potential">Criticality potential</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Partially-reflected-plutonium-sphere.jpeg"><img alt="A stack of square metal plates with a side about 10 inches. In the 3-inch hole in the top plate there is a gray metal ball simulating Pu." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="800" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Partially-reflected-plutonium-sphere.jpeg/220px-Partially-reflected-plutonium-sphere.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Partially-reflected-plutonium-sphere.jpeg/330px-Partially-reflected-plutonium-sphere.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Partially-reflected-plutonium-sphere.jpeg/440px-Partially-reflected-plutonium-sphere.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Partially-reflected-plutonium-sphere.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sphere of simulated plutonium surrounded by neutron-reflecting <a href="/wiki/Tungsten_carbide" title="Tungsten carbide">tungsten carbide</a> blocks in a re-enactment of Harry Daghlian's 1945 experiment</div></div></div>
<p>Care must be taken to avoid the accumulation of amounts of plutonium which approach critical mass, particularly because plutonium's critical mass is only a third of that of uranium-235.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heiserman1992_4-7"><a href="#cite_note-Heiserman1992-4">[4]</a></sup> A critical mass of plutonium emits lethal amounts of neutrons and <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miner1968p546_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miner1968p546-147">[137]</a></sup> Plutonium in solution is more likely to form a critical mass than the solid form due to <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">moderation</a> by the hydrogen in water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC2006p4-27_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2006p4-27-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Criticality_accident" title="Criticality accident">Criticality accidents</a> have occurred in the past, some of them with lethal consequences. Careless handling of <a href="/wiki/Tungsten_carbide" title="Tungsten carbide">tungsten carbide</a> bricks around a 6.2 kg plutonium sphere resulted in a fatal dose of radiation at Los Alamos on August 21, 1945, when scientist <a href="/wiki/Harry_Daghlian" title="Harry Daghlian">Harry Daghlian</a> received a dose estimated to be 5.1 sievert (510 <a href="/wiki/Roentgen_equivalent_man" title="Roentgen equivalent man">rems</a>) and died 25 days later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHunner200485_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHunner200485-149">[139]</a></sup> Nine months later, another Los Alamos scientist, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Slotin" title="Louis Slotin">Louis Slotin</a>, died from a similar accident involving a beryllium reflector and the same plutonium core (the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Demon_core" title="Demon core">demon core</a>") that had previously claimed the life of Daghlian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 1958, during a process of purifying plutonium at Los Alamos, a critical mass was formed in a mixing vessel, which resulted in the death of a chemical operator named <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Kelley_criticality_accident" title="Cecil Kelley criticality accident">Cecil Kelley</a>. Other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents">nuclear accidents</a> have occurred in the Soviet Union, Japan, the United States, and many other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcLaughlinMonahanPruvost200017_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLaughlinMonahanPruvost200017-151">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flammability">Flammability</span></h3>
<p>Metallic plutonium is a fire hazard, especially if the material is finely divided. In a moist environment, plutonium forms hydrides on its surface, which are pyrophoric and may ignite in air at room temperature. Plutonium expands up to 70% in volume as it oxidizes and thus may break its container.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NucSafety_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-NucSafety-33">[32]</a></sup> The radioactivity of the burning material is an additional hazard. <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_oxide" title="Magnesium oxide">Magnesium oxide</a> sand is probably the most effective material for extinguishing a plutonium fire. It cools the burning material, acting as a <a href="/wiki/Heat_sink" title="Heat sink">heat sink</a>, and also blocks off oxygen. Special precautions are necessary to store or handle plutonium in any form; generally a dry <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">inert gas</a> atmosphere is required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NucSafety_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-NucSafety-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[note 11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span></h2>
<p>Around 11 metric tons of plutonium may be possessed by Japan alone, with 36 tons pending return after reprocessing in Britain and France. This is probably enough to make 6,000 atomic bombs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_and_sea">Land and sea</span></h3>
<p>The usual transportation of plutonium is through the more stable plutonium oxide in a sealed package. A typical transport consists of one truck carrying one protected shipping container, holding a number of packages with a total weight varying from 80 to 200 kg of plutonium oxide. A sea shipment may consist of several containers, each of them holding a sealed package.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[144]</a></sup> The United States <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Regulatory_Commission" title="Nuclear Regulatory Commission">Nuclear Regulatory Commission</a> dictates that it must be solid instead of powder if the contents surpass 0.74 <a href="/wiki/Becquerel" title="Becquerel">TBq</a> (20 <a href="/wiki/Curie" title="Curie">Curie</a>) of radioactive activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[145]</a></sup> In a recent example, the ships <a href="/wiki/MV_Pacific_Egret" title="MV Pacific Egret">Pacific Egret</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[146]</a></sup> and Pacific Heron of Pacific Nuclear Transport Ltd. are taking 331 kg (730 lbs) of plutonium to a United States government facility in <a href="/wiki/Savannah_River_Site" title="Savannah River Site">Savannah River</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[148]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air">Air</span></h3>
<p>The U.S. Government air transport regulations permit the transport of plutonium by air, subject to restrictions on other dangerous materials carried on the same flight, packaging requirements, and stowage in the rearmost part of the aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2012 media revealed that plutonium has been flown out of Norway on commercial <a href="/wiki/Passenger_airline" title="Passenger airline">passenger airlines</a>—around every other year—including one time in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-klassekampen1_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-klassekampen1-161">[150]</a></sup> Regulations permit an airplane to transport 15 grams of fissionable material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-klassekampen1_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-klassekampen1-161">[150]</a></sup> Such plutonium transportation is without problems, according to a senior advisor (<i>seniorrådgiver</i>) at <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Statens_str%C3%A5levern&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Statens strålevern (page does not exist)">Statens strålevern</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-klassekampen1_161-2"><a href="#cite_note-klassekampen1-161">[150]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-125"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-125">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">St. Fleur, Nicholas, <a class="external text" href="https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/projects/cp/summer-of-science-2015/latest/new-horizons-pluto-plutonium" rel="nofollow">"The Radioactive Heart of the New Horizons Spacecraft to Pluto"</a>, New York <i>Times</i>, August 7, 2015. The "craft's 125-pound generator [is] called the General Purpose Heat Source-Radioisotope Thermoelectric Generator. [It] was stocked with 24 pounds of plutonium that produced about 240 watts of electricity when it left Earth in 2006, according to Ryan Bechtel, an engineer from the Department of Energy who works on space nuclear power. During the Pluto flyby the battery produced 202 watts, Mr. Bechtel said. The power will continue to decrease as the metal decays, but there is enough of it to command the probe for another 20 years, according to Curt Niebur, a NASA program scientist on the New Horizons mission." Retrieved 2015-08-10.</span>
</li>
